[4:0:344,458] agricultural issues, and services to veterans that

[4:2:344,478] areas. Additionally, this Section includes issues  

[4:3:344,488] that are statewide in nature such as lease/revenue  

[4:4:344,498] issues, bonds, and local government issues.

[1:0:134,39] 8120   Commission on Peace Officer Standards and Training  

[2:0:66,61] The Commission on Peace Officer Standards and Training (POST) is responsible for raising the competence level of law  

[2:1:66,71] enforcement officers in California by establishing minimum selection and training standards, improving management practices,  

[2:2:66,81] and providing financial assistance to local agencies relating to the training of law enforcement officers. POST promotes training  

[2:3:66,91] to improve quality, integrity, accountability, and cooperation; encourages new ideas; explores and uses appropriate  

[2:4:66,101] technologies; and delivers relevant, client-based programs and services.

[4:0:66,273] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:0:66,293] 6500 - STANDARDS  

[6:0:66,313] The Standards Program establishes job-related selection and training standards for peace officers and dispatchers. It also  

[6:1:66,323] provides management consultation to local law enforcement agencies. Activities include development of examinations and  

[6:2:66,334] counseling local law enforcement agencies on ways to improve management practices.  

[7:0:66,354] The Standards Program conducts applied research in the areas of peace officer selection and training, operational procedures  

[7:1:66,364] and program evaluation to meet statutory requirements and to provide management guidance to local law enforcement  

[7:2:66,374] agencies. It also facilitates the development and implementation of new programs for local agencies by serving as a  

[7:3:66,385] clearinghouse of successful program information. Finally, it conducts peace officer feasibility studies.  

[8:0:66,402] 6505 - TRAINING  

[9:0:66,423] The Training Program increases the competency of law enforcement personnel by developing and certifying courses that meet  

[9:1:66,433] identified training needs. This activity requires scheduling and quality control of such courses and assisting law enforcement  

[9:2:66,443] agencies in presenting necessary training and career development programs to their officers. The Commission assesses  

[9:3:66,453] training on a continuing basis to ensure that evolving training needs are met. The curricula cover a wide variety of topics  

[9:4:66,463] necessary to satisfy statutory and regulatory mandates, maintain competence in police work, and address the training needs of  

[9:5:66,474] law enforcement agency personnel. Curricula content is updated regularly. The Training Program also presents advanced  

[9:6:66,484] training for law enforcement supervisors and executives through its Command College and the Supervisory Leadership  

[9:7:66,494] Institute, and for trainers through the Instructor Development Institute. Other specialty programs include the Institute of Criminal  

[9:8:66,504] Investigation.  

[10:0:66,525] Job-related selection and training standards for peace officers and dispatchers, established by the Standards Program, are  

[10:1:66,535] enforced through inspections of local agencies receiving state aid to ensure they are adhering to minimum state standards.  

[11:0:66,553] 6510 - PEACE OFFICER TRAINING  

[12:0:66,573] The Peace Officer Training Program provides financial assistance to participating jurisdictions for instructional costs associated  

[12:1:66,583] with selected training courses. Funding is also provided for the cost of student travel and per diem expenses associated with  

[12:2:66,593] training presentations to enable line officers to receive in-service training in areas of critical need. To encourage and assist local  

[12:3:66,604] law enforcement agencies to meet and maintain minimum standards in the selection and training of law enforcement officers,  

[12:4:66,614] the Commission provides financial assistance to all 58 counties, approximately 346 cities, and numerous specialized districts  

[12:5:66,624] and local agencies which have agreed to meet the Commission's standards.  

[13:0:66,642] 6515 - POST ADMINISTRATION  

[14:0:66,662] The   objective of the California Commission on Peace Officer Standards and Training is to be the leading organization in  

[14:1:66,672] establishing professional standards, assuring top-quality training, and providing effective services to develop and support  

[14:2:66,682] California law enforcement personnel.   California POST utilizes the following   eight bureaus to ensure uniform standards and  

[14:3:66,693] compliance are applied to over 600 law enforcement agencies and 42 basic training academies statewide: Basic Training;  

[14:4:66,703] Management Counseling and   Projects;   Strategic Communications and Research; Learning Technology Resources; Training  

[14:5:66,713] Program Services; Training Delivery and Compliance; Computer Services; and Administrative Services. POST is establishing  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[3:1:66,88] 6530 - STATE PUBLIC DEFENDER  

[4:0:66,109] The objective of the Office of the State Public Defender, upon assignment of cases exclusively from the California Supreme  

[4:1:66,119] Court, is to provide legal services in capital appeals to persons who do not have the financial means to employ private counsel.  

[4:2:66,129] The Office of the State Public Defender is also mandated to provide assistance and training to public defender offices,  

[4:3:66,139] appointed private counsel, and counsel appointed to represent indigent juveniles, and to engage in related efforts for the  

[4:4:66,149] purpose of improving indigent defense. The State Public Defender is headquartered in Oakland, and has a regional office in  

[4:5:66,160] Sacramento.

[7:0:222,368] 8260   California Arts Council  

[7:1:66,390] The California Arts Council (Council) consists of eleven members, nine appointed by the Governor and one each appointed by  

[7:2:66,400] the President pro Tempore of the Senate and the Speaker of the Assembly. The Council establishes general policy and  

[7:3:66,410] approves program allocations.  

[8:0:66,431] The Council recognizes that the Arts are essential for the cultural, educational, social and economic development of California.  

[8:1:66,441] The Council seeks to further its mandates and services to the public through the development of partnerships with the public  

[8:2:66,451] and private sectors and by providing support to the state's non-profit arts and cultural community, which are broad-based and  

[8:3:66,461] extended across the state from its largest metropolitan areas to its most rural areas.  

[9:0:66,482] The Council is statutorily required to:  

[9:1:65,499] • Encourage artistic awareness, participation, and expression among the citizens of California.  

[9:2:65,515] • Help independent local groups develop their own arts programs.  

[9:3:65,532] • Promote the employment of artists and those skilled in crafts in both the public and private sectors.  

[9:4:65,548] • Provide for the exhibition of art works in public buildings throughout California.  

[9:5:65,564] • Enlist the aid of all state agencies in the task of ensuring the fullest expression of our artistic potential.

[1:0:154,92] 8385   California Citizens Compensation Commission  

[1:1:66,114] The California Citizens Compensation Commission is responsible for setting the salaries and benefits for the Governor,  

[1:2:66,124] Lieutenant Governor, State Legislators, Attorney General, Secretary of State, Controller, Treasurer, Superintendent of Public  

[1:3:66,134] Instruction, Insurance Commissioner, and Board of Equalization members.  

[1:4:66,152] The seven-member Commission meets annually, no later than June 30, to determine if any changes should be made to the  

[1:5:66,162] salaries and benefits of the state's elected officials. The decisions of the Commission become effective the first Monday in  

[1:6:66,172] December following the annual meeting.  

[2:0:66,190] The members of the Commission are appointed by the Governor. Three members represent the public, two members represent  

[2:1:66,200] the business community, and two members represent labor organizations. Each member serves a six-year term and the terms  

[2:2:66,210] are staggered.  

[3:0:66,228] The Commission's budget provides for travel expenses and stipends for the annual meeting. Department of Human Resources  

[3:1:66,238] staff provide administrative support to the Commission using its existing resources.

[8:0:183,327] 8420   State Compensation Insurance Fund  

[9:0:66,348] State Compensation Insurance Fund (State Fund) is a public enterprise fund established through legislation that was originally  

[9:1:66,358] enacted in 1913. State Fund is governed by a board of directors with authority comparable to that of the governing body of a  

[9:2:66,369] private insurance carrier. State Fund has its own separate budget process that is presented to its board of directors for  

[9:3:66,379] approval.  

[10:0:66,399] State Fund's purpose is to provide fairly priced workers' compensation insurance, make California workplaces safe, restore  

[10:1:66,409] injured workers, and operate as the continuously available market for workers' compensation insurance for employers located  

[10:2:66,420] in California. Pursuant to the Insurance Code, State Fund is to be self-supporting with revenues from premiums on policies  

[10:3:66,430] written and from investment income. State Fund does not receive any financial support from the State of California, and the  

[10:4:66,440] state is not liable for any obligations of State Fund. Almost all of State Fund's employees are hired through the California civil  

[10:5:66,450] service process and are actually employees of the state.  

[11:0:66,471] State Fund is considered a related organization to the State of California because State Fund was created by the state and  

[11:1:66,481] because the Governor appoints a voting majority of State Fund's board of directors. However, the State of California is not  

[11:2:66,491] financially liable or responsible for the operations or solvency of State Fund.  

[12:0:66,511] Most of the state agencies and departments of the State of California are legally uninsured for workers' compensation coverage  

[12:1:66,522] and benefits for injured employees. Separate from its issuance of workers' compensation insurance policies to private  

[12:2:66,532] employers, State Fund has been engaged by the California Department of Human Resources to provide workers'  

[12:3:66,542] compensation claims administration services for these legally uninsured departments, agencies, boards, commissions, and  

[12:4:66,552] other subdivisions of the State of California under a Master Services Agreement. Under this agreement, the state provides a  

[12:5:66,562] deposit out of which State Fund pays compensation benefits to injured state workers and medical benefits to health providers,  

[12:6:66,573] and State Fund is reimbursed by state agencies for benefit amounts paid plus a service fee equal to the cost of these services.  

[13:0:66,593] For informational purposes only, the table further below   provides summary data on actual and estimated workers'  

[13:1:66,603] compensation claims costs paid by state agencies. All workers' compensation costs are included in the individual budgets of  

[13:2:66,613] the responsible state agencies. Costs also include administrative charges under the Master Services Agreement.

[3:1:66,85] 6560 - WORKERS' COMPENSATION BENEFITS

[11:0:184,492] 8570   Department of Food and Agriculture  

[11:1:66,514] The California Department of Food and Agriculture serves the citizens of California by promoting and protecting a safe, healthy  

[11:2:66,524] food supply; and enhancing local and global agricultural trade, through efficient management, innovation, and sound science,  

[11:3:66,534] with a commitment to environmental stewardship.  

[12:0:66,554] The goals of the California Department of Food and Agriculture are to:  

[12:1:65,572] • Promote and protect the diverse local and global marketability of the California agricultural brand, which represents superior  

[12:2:76,582] quality, value, and safety.  

[13:0:65,598] • Optimize fiscal resources through collaboration, innovation, and process improvements.  

[14:0:65,615] • Connect rural and urban communities by supporting and participating in educational programs that emphasize a mutual  

[14:1:76,625] appreciation of the value of diverse food and agricultural production systems.  

[14:2:65,641] • Improve regulatory efficiency through proactive coordination with stakeholders.  

[14:3:65,657] • Invest in employee development and succession planning efforts.  

[15:0:66,675] Because the California Department of Food and Agriculture's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the  

[15:1:66,685] Department has a capital outlay program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see  

[15:2:66,695] "Infrastructure Overview."  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[6:0:66,238] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[7:0:65,258] • Sustainable Agriculture Package—The Budget includes $242 million General Fund in 2022-23 as part of a $1.1 billion  

[7:1:76,268] package for investments that support the agriculture sector. This includes funding for programs that create a healthy,  

[7:2:76,278] resilient, and equitable food system; promote climate smart agriculture to support long term sustainability and resilience; and  

[7:3:76,288] address economic recovery and high-road job growth.  

[8:0:65,304] • Water Resilience and Drought Package—The Budget includes $50 million General Fund in 2022-23 as part of a $5.2 billion  

[8:1:76,315] package to support water resilience and drought response efforts. This includes funding for the State Water Efficiency  

[8:2:76,325] and   Enhancement Program.  

[9:0:65,341] • Drought Resilience and Response Package—The Budget includes $40.2 million California Emergency Fund in 2021-22 for  

[9:1:76,351] the Water Efficiency Technical Assistance Program and direct relief for small farmers, as part of a $2.7 billion package of  

[9:2:76,361] investments to support the state’s response to continued drought conditions. Of the total $2.7 billion, $1.5 billion will be  

[9:3:76,372] allocated at a later time.  

[10:0:65,388] • Farm to School Program—The Budget includes an additional $30 million General Fund in 2022-23 to expand the California  

[10:1:76,398] Farm to School Incubator Grant Program to serve additional schools across the state and $2.9 million General Fund ongoing  

[10:2:76,408] to support 16 regional Farm to School Network coordinators and market specialists.

[6:0:66,568] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:0:66,587] 6570 - AGRICULTURAL PLANT AND ANIMAL HEALTH; PEST PREVENTION; FOOD SAFETY SERVICES  

[8:0:66,608] The objective of this program is to prevent the introduction and establishment of serious plant and animal pests and diseases to  

[8:1:66,618] California, promote judicious antimicrobial use and stewardship   and protect the safety of California's dairy, eggs and meat  

[8:2:66,628] products that are exempt from federal inspection. In particular, the program is focused on pests and diseases that can: (1) be  

[8:3:66,638] transmitted to humans, (2) inflict catastrophic financial loss on California's farmers, ranchers, and associated businesses,  

[8:4:66,649] (3) have severe negative impacts on the environment, or (4) adversely affect the supply of agricultural products to the  

[8:5:66,659] consumer.  

[9:0:66,677] 6575 - MARKETING; COMMODITIES AND AGRICULTURAL SERVICES  

[10:0:66,697] California agriculture produces over 400 different crops, which enter state, national, and international commerce. The  

[10:1:66,707] objectives of this program are to assure orderly domestic and international marketing of safe and quality agricultural  

[11:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] commodities, promote consumer protection, increase food access, ensure fair pricing practices, oversee industry-supported  

[3:1:66,79] grading services, and maintain standards of measurement which provide a basis of value comparison, fair competition in the  

[3:2:66,89] marketplace, and establish quality standards for conventional and alternative fuels and automotive products.  

[3:3:66,107] Additionally, this program provides support to governmental agencies that work to protect the nation's food supply and the  

[3:4:66,117] environment by monitoring for chemical contaminants.  

[3:5:66,135] 6580 - ASSISTANCE TO FAIRS AND COUNTY AGRICULTURAL ACTIVITIES  

[4:0:66,155] This program provides fiscal and policy oversight to the network of California fairs.  

[5:0:66,175] The State has a network of 79 fairs including county fairs, citrus fruit fairs, District Agricultural Associations and the California  

[5:1:66,186] State Fair (an independent state agency). State oversight of these local fairs includes attendance of board meetings and  

[5:2:66,196] periodic financial reviews and audits.  

[5:3:66,214] 6590 - GENERAL AGRICULTURAL ACTIVITIES  

[6:0:66,234] This program provides fiscal and policy oversight   for federal grants that promote California agriculture, and for   CDFA  

[6:1:66,244] Greenhouse Gas Reduction Program activities   that are designed to reduce greenhouse gas emissions in agriculture.  

[7:0:66,265] In addition, this program serves as the central point of contact for logistical coordination of all departmental resources, provides  

[7:1:66,275] industry and agency coordination on environmental issues affecting agriculture, and provides centralized communications to  

[7:2:66,285] California's agricultural industry, including County Agricultural Commissioners and the statewide fairgrounds.  

[8:0:66,305] This program also partially reimburses County Agricultural Commissioners' Offices for carrying out agricultural programs  

[8:1:66,316] authorized by the Food and Agricultural Code under the supervision of CDFA.  

[8:2:66,333] 9900 - EXECUTIVE, MANAGEMENT AND ADMINISTRATIVE SERVICES  

[9:0:66,354] Executive and Management Services include the executive leadership of the Secretary's Office. The Secretary's Office sets  

[9:1:66,364] priorities and policies to protect, support, and promote agriculture in the State of California, and helps to protect the health and  

[9:2:66,374] welfare of the public and the environment.  

[10:0:66,394] Administrative Services provides centralized administrative support to the Department through fiscal operations, employee-

[10:1:66,405] employer relations, personnel management, employee development, and general business services.

[5:0:66,480] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[5:1:66,499] The California Department of Food and Agriculture's (CDFA) facilities support its operations serving the citizens of California by  

[5:2:66,509] promoting and protecting a safe and healthy food supply and enhancing local and global agricultural trade through efficient  

[5:3:66,519] management, innovation, and sound science, with a commitment to environmental stewardship. These facilities are in various  

[5:4:66,529] locations in Arizona, Hawaii, and throughout California. The CDFA rents or owns approximately 710,000 square feet of  

[5:5:66,540] facilities, including 41 field offices, 11 laboratories, 16 border protection stations, 9 employee residences, and a headquarters in  

[5:6:66,550] Sacramento.

[11:0:185,583] 8620   Fair Political Practices Commission  

[12:0:66,605] The Fair Political Practices Commission has primary responsibility for the impartial administration, implementation, and  

[12:1:66,615] enforcement of the Political Reform Act of 1974, as amended by the voters and Legislature. The purpose of the Act is to restore  

[12:2:66,625] confidence in governmental processes. The major objectives of the Commission are to:  

[12:3:65,643] • Provide education about the Act and its requirements to the public and the regulated community including public officials,  

[12:4:76,653] candidates, and lobbyists, and assist with compliance.  

[13:0:65,669] • Ensure that election campaign contribution and expenditure data is fully and accurately disclosed so that the voters may be  

[13:1:76,679] fully informed.  

[14:0:65,696] • Enforce the provisions of the Act and regulations fairly and with due process.  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:208,100] 8660   Public Utilities Commission  

[4:0:66,121] The California Public Utilities Commission (PUC) regulates privately-owned electric, natural gas, telecommunications, water,  

[4:1:66,131] railroad, rail transit, and passenger transportation companies, in addition to authorizing cable and video franchises. The PUC  

[4:2:66,141] empowers California in the areas of telecommunications and utilities through assuring services are reliable, clean, and safe;  

[4:3:66,152] providing for critical services and infrastructure; designing rates that are fair and reasonable; and protecting the interests of  

[4:4:66,162] consumers and ratepayers.

[6:0:66,387] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:0:66,406] 6680 - REGULATION OF UTILITIES  

[8:0:66,424] Californians spend more than $50 billion annually for services from industries regulated by the PUC. This includes 6 electricity  

[8:1:66,434] utilities, 1,012 telecommunications carriers, 120 water and sewer utilities, 5 natural gas utilities with approximately 10.8 million  

[8:2:66,444] customers, 4 gas facility owners, and more than 3,155 small mobile home park and propane operators.  

[9:0:66,462] The PUC oversees the safety of electric, communications, natural gas, and propane gas utility systems. Safety functions  

[9:1:66,472] include both backward-looking investigations and analysis of safety incidents and utility performance, and forward-looking risk  

[9:2:66,482] analytics to mitigate emerging threats and identify necessary regulatory rule reform. The PUC performs operation and  

[9:3:66,492] maintenance audits, outage inspections, mitigation reviews, and investigations of incidents at electric generation facilities and  

[9:4:66,503] coordinates closely with state public safety agencies to further resilience initiatives. The PUC’s safety oversight includes  

[9:5:66,513] enforcing public utility wildfire safety and Public Safety Power Shutoffs (PSPS) violations. It functions as the lead investigator  

[9:6:66,523] for utility incidents relating to wildfire events and recommends policy to strengthen PSPS guidelines. In addition to the PUC  

[9:7:66,533] opening formal investigations, the PUC imposes fines for natural gas, electric infrastructure, and wildfire safety-related  

[9:8:66,543] violations.  

[10:0:66,561] The PUC conducts reviews of utility operations every four years to determine just and reasonable rates for energy services.  

[10:1:66,571] The PUC provides guidelines for investor-owned utilities—and to a lesser extent, as directed by statute, an increasing number  

[10:2:66,581] of Community Choice Aggregators, Energy Service Providers, and Core Transport Agents—to follow when purchasing  

[10:3:66,592] electricity and natural gas on behalf of their customers to ensure that sufficient amounts of electricity are procured when and  

[10:4:66,602] where needed in the state.  

[11:0:66,620] Environmental protection and the impact of climate change are critical factors in PUC regulation. The PUC is charged with  

[11:1:66,630] implementing and overseeing key components of Chapter 547, Statutes of 2015 (SB 350); Chapter 312, Statutes of 2018 (SB  

[11:2:66,640] 100); and other climate change legislation, including promoting ratepayer-funded energy efficiency programs, Integrated  

[11:3:66,650] Resource Planning, the state's mandated renewable energy programs, programs for disadvantaged communities, electric  

[11:4:66,660] vehicle programs, and other clean energy programs.  

[12:0:66,678] The PUC oversees programs and regulations to promote both natural gas and electricity reliability and resilience.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 47 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] GENERAL GOVERNMENT 

[1:0:416,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — GG   45 

[2:0:173,32] 8660   Public Utilities Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The PUC establishes requirements for energy programs for low-income ratepayers, including programs providing rate  

[3:1:66,79] discounts, financial assistance with energy bills, and the Energy Savings Assistance Program, which provides no-cost  

[3:2:66,89] weatherization services to customers who meet low-income eligibility criteria.  

[4:0:66,107] The PUC conducts and manages environmental reviews pursuant to the California Environmental Quality Act for transmission,  

[4:1:66,117] telecommunications, and other infrastructure projects.  

[5:0:66,135] The PUC develops and implements policies for the rapidly changing communications and broadband markets, including  

[5:1:66,145] monitoring competitive markets for proper operation, enforcing carrier service standards for telecommunication services,  

[5:2:66,155] regulating rural telecommunication rates, protecting consumers against carrier abuses and fraud by applying licensing  

[5:3:66,165] requirements and rules for offering and terminating telecommunication services, and managing efficient deployment of area  

[5:4:66,175] codes and telephone numbers.  

[6:0:66,193] The PUC is responsible for ensuring that California's investor-owned water utilities deliver clean, safe, and reliable water to  

[6:1:66,203] their customers at reasonable rates. The PUC's water utility work includes investigating water and sewer service quality,  

[6:2:66,214] promoting water conservation and metering, improving low-income programs, analyzing and processing rate change requests,  

[6:3:66,224] and tracking and certifying compliance with PUC requirements.  

[7:0:66,241] The Public Advisor's Office provides procedural information, advice, and assistance to individuals and groups interested in  

[7:1:66,252] participating in PUC proceedings and advises the Commission on procedural matters related to public participation in  

[7:2:66,262] proceedings.  

[8:0:66,280] The Consumer Affairs Branch (CAB) responds to questions regarding utility service and bills and assists consumers who have  

[8:1:66,290] unresolved disputes with regulated utilities through an informal process. CAB also provides data on the consumer protection  

[8:2:66,300] issues it handles to stakeholders and decision makers to inform policy making. A Supplier Diversity program promotes and  

[8:3:66,310] monitors supplier diversity in procurement by utilities and oversees a certification clearinghouse.  

[9:0:66,328] 6685 - UNIVERSAL SERVICE TELECOMMUNICATIONS PROGRAMS 

[10:0:66,346] The PUC oversees telecommunication-based universal service programs (Universal Lifeline Telephone Service Trust Fund,  

[10:1:66,356] California Teleconnect Fund, Deaf and Disabled Telecommunications Program, California Advanced Services Fund, and two  

[10:2:66,366] California High Cost Funds), all of which provide needed services to consumers and communities. The objectives of these  

[10:3:66,376] "universal service" programs include: (1) ensuring basic telephone service is available to all Californians; (2) improving access  

[10:4:66,386] to broadband technology to bridge the digital divide; and (3) ensuring consumers have access to sufficient information to make  

[10:5:66,397] informed choices about basic service and universal lifeline telephone services.  

[11:0:66,416] The California LifeLine Program provides discounted basic residential wireless and wireline telephone service to qualified low-

[11:1:66,427] income households. The California Teleconnect Fund Program provides discounted telecommunication services to qualifying  

[11:2:66,437] schools, libraries, hospitals, and community-based organizations. The Deaf and Disabled Telecommunications Program  

[11:3:66,447] primarily provides equipment and telephone access services to people with hearing and other disabilities. The California  

[11:4:66,457] Advanced Services Fund Program mainly encourages the deployment of broadband infrastructure in unserved and  

[11:5:66,467] underserved rural and urban areas of California. The California High Cost Fund Programs provide subsidies to carriers  

[11:6:66,478] obligated to provide telephone service to customers residing in rural and high-cost areas.  

[12:0:66,497] 6690 - REGULATION OF TRANSPORTATION 

[13:0:66,515] The PUC oversees the safety of railroads, rail transit systems, rail crossings, and other forms of motor conveyance in  

[13:1:66,525] California. All railroads, seven major rail transit agencies, six smaller transit systems, and more than 13,500 public and private  

[13:2:66,535] crossings are subject to PUC regulation and inspection. For traditional heavy rail, the PUC's specially trained and federally-

[13:3:66,546] certified inspectors inspect all tracks (annually), all train equipment and facilities (thrice annually), and all signals and  

[13:4:66,556] appurtenances; investigate all rail accidents resulting in loss of life and property damage; and ensure safe transport of  

[13:5:66,566] hazardous materials by rail.  

[14:0:66,586] For rail transit systems, PUC inspectors and engineers conduct safety inspections of track, vehicles, signals and train control,  

[14:1:66,596] and operations, as well as overall agency triennial safety audits. The PUC also has exclusive authority to approve or  

[14:2:66,606] disapprove all highway-rail crossings of railroad or rail transit tracks, as well as crossing safety treatments deployed at each  

[14:3:66,616] crossing. Additionally, the PUC jointly (with Caltrans) administers the state's annual allocation of federal funds for crossing  

[14:4:66,627] hazard elimination projects and the state's grade separation program, which assists localities with the high cost of grade-

[14:5:66,637] separating highway/rail crossings.  

[15:0:66,657] The transportation program also oversees passenger carriers, including privately-owned for-hire passenger transportation  

[15:1:66,667] companies (e.g., limousines, airport shuttles, transportation network companies, and charter and scheduled bus operators) and  

[15:2:66,677] privately-owned water ferries. The program regulates rates and services, issues certificates and other licenses, enforces safety  

[15:3:66,687] standards and insurance requirements, and protects consumers from for-hire passenger carriers operating illegally.  

[16:0:66,707] 6695 - PUBLIC ADVOCATE'S OFFICE  

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:173,32] 8660   Public Utilities Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The mandate and function of the Public Advocate's Office is defined in Public Utilities Code Section 309.5. The Public  

[3:1:66,79] Advocate's Office is charged with representing and advocating on behalf of public utility customers and subscribers in all  

[3:2:66,89] significant proceedings within the PUC's jurisdiction.  

[4:0:66,107] Chapter 856, Statutes of 1996 (SB 960), codified the Public Advocate's Office with a director appointed by and serving at the  

[4:1:66,117] pleasure of the Governor and charged the Public Advocate's Office with representing ratepayer interests in PUC proceedings.  

[4:2:66,127] Chapter 1005, Statutes of 1999 (AB 1658), directed funds from the Public Utilities Commission Utilities Reimbursement  

[4:3:66,137] Account to be transferred in the annual budget act to the Public Utilities Commission Public Advocate's Office Account. The law  

[4:4:66,148] further required that "funds in the Public Utilities Commission Public Advocate's Office Account shall be utilized exclusively by  

[4:5:66,158] the division in the performance of its duties as determined by the director" of the Public Advocates Office.  

[4:6:66,175] Chapter 440, Statutes of 2001 (SB 201), removed the limitation that the Public Advocate's Office only represent ratepayers in  

[4:7:66,186] PUC proceedings. SB 201 extended the Public Advocate's Office representation to other venues such as, but not limited to, the  

[4:8:66,196] Energy Commission, Air Resources Board, California Independent System Operator, and legislative hearings.

[6:0:72,226] 8780   Milton Marks "Little Hoover" Commission on California State Governmental  

[6:1:228,241] Organization and Economy  

[6:2:66,262] The Milton Marks "Little Hoover" Commission on California State Government Organization and Economy (Commission) is the  

[6:3:66,272] state's only independent and citizen-based oversight mechanism. It conducts four to five comprehensive reviews of executive  

[6:4:66,283] branch programs, departments, and agencies each year and recommends ways to improve performance by increasing  

[6:5:66,293] efficiency and effectiveness with existing resources. The Commission is statutorily responsible for analyzing and making  

[6:6:66,303] recommendations to the Legislature on all Governor reorganization plans.  

[7:0:66,323] The Commission is composed of two members of the Senate, two members of the Assembly, and nine unpaid citizen members  

[7:1:66,334] - five appointed by the Governor and four appointed by the Legislature.  

[8:0:66,354] Because of the Commission's independence, the Legislature charged it with oversight of the California State Auditor, including  

[8:1:66,364] contracting with an independent auditor for annually examining the State Audit Fund.

[7:0:190,318] 8815   Youth Empowerment Commission  

[7:1:66,339] The California Youth Empowerment Commission is an advisory commission that provides meaningful opportunities for actual  

[7:2:66,350] civic engagement to improve the quality of life for California’s disconnected and disadvantaged youth. The Commission  

[7:3:66,360] examines and discusses policy and fiscal issues affecting the interests, needs, and conditions of the youth of California,  

[7:4:66,370] formally advises and makes recommendations to the Legislature, Superintendent of Public Instruction, and Governor on  

[7:5:66,380] specific legislative and fiscal issues affecting youth, and consults with existing local-level youth advisory commissions and  

[7:6:66,390] community-based, grassroots youth-led organizations for input and potential solutions on issues related to youth. The  

[7:7:66,401] Commission was established by Chapter 660, Statutes of 2021, and will remain in effect under current law until January 1,  

[7:8:66,411] 2027.  

[8:0:66,428] The 13 members of the Commission, all of whom must be between 14 and 25 years of age, include 11 members appointed by  

[8:1:66,439] the Governor, 1 member appointed by the Senate Committee on Rules, and 1 member appointed by the Speaker of the  

[8:2:66,449] Assembly.

[5:0:66,409] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,429] 6730 - ADMINISTRATION, LEGISLATION, RESEARCH, AND INFORMATION  

[7:0:66,456] The Commission implements its mandate by identifying and advancing public policy that need legislative or executive action;  

[7:1:66,467] maintaining and distributing information to the public on California programs and policies designed to impact the status of  

[7:2:66,477] women and girls; developing and implementing programs aligned with the organizational mission; developing and maintaining a  

[7:3:66,487] liaison with other government agencies and advisory bodies providing technical and consultative assistance to government,  

[7:4:66,497] non-profit and for-profit entities seeking to assist women; developing and maintaining a liaison with local commissions on  

[7:5:66,507] women; acting as granting and supervisory body for programs and special projects significantly impacting the status of women  

[7:6:66,518] and girls. The Commission's priority issues include, but are not limited to, examining any laws, practices, or conditions  

[7:7:66,528] concerning or affecting women and girls which impose special limitations or burdens upon them or upon society, or which limit  

[7:8:66,538] or tend to limit opportunities available to women and girls generally and specifically as it relates to economic security and  

[7:9:66,548] opportunity, reproductive justice, gender equity in the media, educational needs of women and girls, gender in the workplace  

[7:10:66,558] and employment, equal pay for women, health and safety of women and girls, and women in the military, women veterans, and  

[7:11:66,569] families.

[1:0:182,69] 8830   California Law Revision Commission  

[2:0:66,91] The California Law Revision Commission (Commission) has the responsibility to make a continuing substantive review of  

[2:1:66,101] California statutory and decisional law, to recommend legislation to make needed reforms, and to make recommendations to  

[2:2:66,111] the Governor and Legislature for revision of the law on major topics (as assigned by the Legislature) that require detailed study  

[2:3:66,121] and cannot easily be handled in the ordinary legislative process.  

[3:0:66,142] The Commission consists of seven gubernatorial appointees plus one Senator, one Assembly Member, and the Legislative  

[3:1:66,152] Counsel. The Commission's work is independent, nonpartisan, and objective.  

[4:0:66,169] The Commission also administers and provides staff support for the Committee on Revision of the Penal Code. The Committee  

[4:1:66,180] is responsible for studying the Penal Code and   recommending reforms to simplify and rationalize California’s criminal law and  

[4:2:66,190] procedures.   The Committee consists of five gubernatorial appointees, one Senator, and one Assembly Member.

[0:0:66,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — GG   74   GENERAL GOVERNMENT 

[1:0:197,69] 8855   California State Auditor's Office  

[2:0:66,91] The California State Auditor (State Auditor) promotes the effective and efficient administration and management of public funds  

[2:1:66,101] and programs by providing citizens and government officials nonpartisan, accurate, and objective assessments of state and  

[2:2:66,111] local governments' financial and operational activities. As the independent auditor, the state law exclusively grants the State  

[2:3:66,121] Auditor full access to all records of state and local agencies, special districts, school districts, and any publicly-created entity.  

[2:4:66,131] The State Auditor conducts performance, financial, or compliance audits that are either mandated by statute or requested by  

[2:5:66,142] the Legislature through the Joint Legislative Audit Committee (JLAC). Further, the State Auditor is responsible for annually  

[3:0:66,152] conducting California's statewide Single Audit - a combination of the independent audit of the State's basic financial statements  

[3:1:66,162] and the independent audit of numerous federal programs administered by California. The Single Audit is federally required as a  

[3:2:66,172] condition for California to receive billions in federal funds each year. In 2011-12, the Legislature enacted a program that  

[3:3:66,182] requires the State Auditor to conduct pilot audits of the procurement practices of six trial courts in 2012-13 and thereafter  

[3:4:66,193] biennially audit the procurement practices of the Administrative Office of the Courts and five judicial branch entities - which  

[3:5:66,203] includes trial courts, Appellate Courts, and the Habeas Corpus Resource Center.  

[4:0:66,223] The State Auditor also identifies statewide issues or specific government entities as being at-high risk for waste, fraud, abuse,  

[4:1:66,233] and mismanagement or that have major challenges related to efficiency and effectiveness. The State Auditor has the authority  

[4:2:66,244] to conduct evaluations of those issues and entities identified as being high risk. Similar to the state high-risk program, Chapter  

[4:3:66,254] 451, Statutes of 2011 (AB 187), also authorizes the State Auditor, to the extent resources are available, to identify local  

[4:4:66,264] government agencies - including city, county, or special districts or publicly-created entities - as being at-high risk for the  

[5:0:66,274] potential of waste, fraud, abuse, or mismanagement or that have major challenges associated with their economy, efficiency, or  

[5:1:66,284] effectiveness. Further, if approved by the JLAC, the State Auditor can audit these identified local entities.  

[6:0:66,305] Additionally, under the California Whistleblower Protection Act, the State Auditor's office has broad authority to perform  

[7:0:66,315] independent investigations into allegations of improper governmental activities by employees at state agencies and courts. The  

[7:1:66,325] State Auditor's office administers California's Whistleblower Hotline that enables the public to report allegations of improper  

[7:2:66,335] acts committed by the courts, state agencies, departments, or employees, as defined by law.  

[8:0:66,356] The Voters FIRST Act, approved by voters in November 2008 as Proposition 11, requires the State Auditor to initiate an  

[8:1:66,366] application process for selecting the members of a 14-member Citizens Redistricting Commission (Commission) every 10  

[8:2:66,376] years. The State Auditor develops regulations to implement the application and selection process of the Commission and  

[8:3:66,386] conducts comprehensive outreach to prospective applicants to serve on the Commission.

[3:0:66,69] 6770 - STATE BUDGET  

[4:0:66,89] To ensure the financial integrity of the state through the planned allocation of state resources, the Department of Finance  

[4:1:66,99] advises the Governor on the fiscal condition of the state and prepares the annual Governor's Budget. The Governor's Budget  

[4:2:66,109] contains past year actual expenditures and revenues, current year estimated expenditures and revenues, and proposed  

[4:3:66,120] expenditures and estimated revenues for the next fiscal year. After the budget is enacted, the Department of Finance assists in  

[4:4:66,130] the administration of the budget. The Department of Finance also conducts performance reviews of state agencies and  

[4:5:66,140] programs, recommends ways to lower the costs of state government and to better serve the public within existing resources,  

[4:6:66,150] and provides fiscal analyses of bills introduced in the Legislature and makes recommendations to the Governor. Federal Funds  

[4:7:66,160] Accountability and Cost Tracking functions support the coordination of the tracking, reporting, transparency, and accountability  

[4:8:66,171] for federal stimulus and disaster recovery funds, while also performing certain federal disaster cost recovery activities in  

[4:9:66,181] coordination with the California Office of Emergency Services. Basic and applied economic and tax research functions support  

[4:10:66,191] the revenue estimates required for the development of the state financial plan, for the analysis of financial legislation, and the  

[4:11:66,201] evaluation of economic trends or other fiscal developments which affect the state. Basic and applied demographic research  

[4:12:66,211] functions provide data to support the workload estimates of state agencies.  

[4:13:66,229] 6775 - FINANCIAL INFORMATION SYSTEM FOR CALIFORNIA (FI$Cal) PROJECT SUPPORT  

[5:0:66,250] The Department of Finance is one of four partner agencies responsible for the development, implementation, utilization, and  

[5:1:66,260] maintenance of an integrated statewide financial and administrative information technology system, FI$Cal. This program  

[5:2:66,270] includes Department of Finance subject-matter experts assigned to the FI$Cal Project.  

[5:3:66,288] 6780 - STATE AUDITS AND EVALUATIONS  

[6:0:66,308] The Department of Finance systematically reviews and evaluates state-administered and financed programs. The Office of  

[6:1:66,318] State Audits and Evaluations assists the Director of Finance in fulfilling the statutory responsibilities for supervision over all  

[6:2:66,328] matters concerning the financial and business policies of the state by conducting financial audits and by performing critical  

[6:3:66,339] examinations of state programs and policies including the oversight activities associated with the dissolution of redevelopment  

[6:4:66,349] agencies.  

[7:0:66,367] 6785 - STATEWIDE ACCOUNTING POLICIES, CONSULTING AND TRAINING  

[8:0:66,387] The Department of Finance is responsible for devising and maintaining a modern and uniform system of statewide accounting. 

[8:1:66,397] The Fiscal Systems and Consulting Unit sets statewide fiscal and accounting policies and procedures.   The unit provides fiscal  

[8:2:66,407] and accounting training, advice, and consulting services to departments to ensure that the state's assets are protected, and  

[8:3:66,418] that   accurate and timely financial information is maintained.   The Department of Finance participates in the development of  

[8:4:66,428] statewide financial systems.

[5:0:164,456] 8880   Financial Information System for California  

[6:0:66,478] The Department of FI$Cal operates the statewide financial system to enable California to perform budgeting, accounting,  

[6:1:66,488] procurement and cash management functions transparently and efficiently. The Department of FI$Cal is responsible for  

[7:0:66,498] supporting the Financial Information System for California (FI$Cal) Project, including supporting the system's customers and  

[7:1:66,508] stakeholders.  

[8:0:66,529] The FI$Cal Project is a partnership of four control agencies: the Department of Finance, the State Controller's Office, the State  

[8:1:66,539] Treasurer's Office, and the Department of General Services. FI$Cal provides the state with a single integrated financial  

[9:0:66,549] management system that encompasses budgeting, accounting, procurement, cash management, and financial management  

[9:1:66,559] and reporting. FI$Cal, through the adoption of best business practices, reengineers business processes; improves efficiency;  

[9:2:66,569] enhances decision making and resource management; and provides reliable, accessible, and timely statewide financial  

[10:0:66,580] information allowing the state to be more transparent. FI$Cal further enables transparency through its Open FI$Cal website,  

[11:0:66,590] which was implemented in 2018 pursuant to Government Code section 11862. The website will continue to expand providing  

[11:1:66,600] the public with access to a growing number of the state's non-confidential expenditures.

[1:0:196,69] 8885   Commission on State Mandates  

[2:0:66,91] The Commission on State Mandates timely renders sound, quasi-judicial decisions, in compliance with Article XIII B, Section 6  

[2:1:66,101] of the California Constitution, resolving disputes regarding reimbursement for state-mandated local programs and relieving  

[2:2:66,111] unnecessary congestion of the courts. Its mission is to fairly and impartially hear and determine matters filed by state and local  

[2:3:66,121] government; resolve complex legal questions in a deliberative and timely manner; and produce clear, well-reasoned, and lawful  

[2:4:66,131] decisions.  

[3:0:66,149] The Commission is a quasi-judicial body consisting of the Director of Finance, the State Controller, the State Treasurer, the  

[3:1:66,159] Director of the Office of Planning and Research, a public member with experience in public finance, and two members from the  

[3:2:66,169] categories of city council member, county supervisor, or school district governing board member, appointed by the Governor  

[3:3:66,180] and approved by the Senate.

[4:0:66,248] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[4:1:66,268] 6900 - ADMINISTRATION  

[5:0:66,285] The Commission's statutory responsibilities are to hear and decide:  

[5:1:65,303] • Test claims of local agencies and school districts that allege the existence of reimbursable state-mandated programs; and if  

[5:2:76,313] a test claim is approved, adopt parameters and guidelines for claiming reimbursement and prepare a statewide cost  

[5:3:76,324] estimate.  

[6:0:65,340] • Claims alleging that the State Controller's Office has incorrectly reduced payments to local agencies and school districts.  

[6:1:65,356] • Requests for mandate redeterminations that allege the state's liability for a mandate has been modified based on a  

[6:2:76,366] subsequent change in law.  

[7:0:65,382] • Requests to review the claiming instructions issued by the State Controller.  

[8:0:65,399] • Requests to determine whether a mandated program, for which appropriations have been made by the Legislature in any  

[8:1:76,409] three consecutive years, should be included in the State Mandates Apportionment System.  

[8:2:65,425] • Existence of significant financial distress for applicant counties seeking to reduce their General Assistance Aid payments.

[9:0:231,368] 8940   Military Department  

[10:0:66,389] The Military Department is responsible for the command, leadership and management of the California National Guard, Youth  

[10:1:66,399] and Community Programs, State Guard and the Naval Militia. The California Military Department, under proper authority,  

[10:2:66,409] organizes, resources, and trains forces with unique capabilities to serve the community, state, and nation. The purpose of the  

[10:3:66,420] California National Guard is to provide mission ready forces to the federal government as directed by the President, emergency  

[10:4:66,430] public safety support to civil authorities as directed by the Governor, and support to our member's families and to the  

[10:5:66,440] community. With an authorized strength of approximately 19,000, the Army National Guard and Air National Guard are  

[10:6:66,450] organized, manned, and funded in accordance with federal Departments of the Army and Air Force regulatory guidance.  

[11:0:66,471] The Military Department Youth and Community Program serves California communities and families by delivering national  

[11:1:66,481] level, high quality educational support programs, in partnership with the educational community, within a military, academic  

[11:2:66,491] structured environment.  

[12:0:66,511] Because the Military Department's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay  

[12:1:66,522] program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[9:0:66,349] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[9:1:65,368] • Enhancing and Expanding Fire Crews: Task Force Rattlesnake—The Budget includes $39.9 million General Fund in  

[9:2:76,378] 2022-23, and $41.3 million annually thereafter, to increase the number of Military-staffed fire crews from 10 to 14, and to  

[9:3:76,388] make crews available year-round for both wildfire activities and fuel reduction efforts.  

[9:4:65,404] • Drug Interdiction—The Budget includes $15 million General Fund in 2022-23 and $15 million in 2023-24 to support the  

[9:5:76,415] National Guard’s drug interdiction efforts in border regions, with a particular focus on assisting federal, state, local, and tribal  

[9:6:76,425] law enforcement agencies in combating fentanyl.  

[10:0:65,441] • New Consolidated Headquarter Complex Operations and Support—The Budget includes $3.9 million General Fund in  

[10:1:76,451] 2022-23, and $4.9 million annually thereafter, to operate and maintain the new Consolidated Headquarters Complex located  

[10:2:76,461] in Rancho Cordova.  

[11:0:65,478] • California Cadet Corps—The Budget includes $3.1 million General Fund in 2022-23, and $4.3 million annually thereafter, to  

[11:1:76,488] expand the California Cadet Corps program. The program currently supports approximately 90 schools and 6,000 cadets  

[11:2:76,498] annually, and this funding is expected to enable the program to support about 135 schools and 9,095 cadets annually by  

[11:3:76,508] 2026-27.  

[12:0:65,524] • State Information Technology Network: Phase 3—The Budget includes $3.1 million General Fund in 2022-23, and $2.1  

[12:1:76,535] million annually thereafter, for the development, implementation, and maintenance of Phase 3 of the new information  

[12:2:76,545] technology network for state operations.  

[13:0:65,561] • State Personnel Staffing—The Budget includes $479,000 ongoing General Fund to address increased administrative  

[13:1:76,571] requirements and demands on state personnel resulting from processing deployments and transfers of servicemembers.

[6:0:66,465] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:1:66,485] 6911 - NATIONAL GUARD  

[7:0:66,505] The Military Department will maximize the readiness of the California National Guard's Soldiers and Airmen, along with our  

[7:1:66,515] State Guard. Army National Guard support plans include a community-based land force, logistics, communications, law  

[7:2:66,525] enforcement and other specialized support. Air National Guard support plans include rescue, air defense, airlift and unmanned  

[7:3:66,536] aerial systems, space, intelligence, communications, and other specialized services. The Office of the Adjutant General  

[7:4:66,546] element governs the joint activities and performance of the Military Department in areas such as personnel and fiscal resource  

[7:5:66,556] management, judicial affairs, internal controls, facility management, and information technology. The Military Civil Support  

[7:6:66,566] element provides liaison and coordination with federal, state, and local agencies so that mutual understanding and unity of  

[7:7:66,576] purpose is assured during an emergency. The objectives of this element are to plan, prepare, and train for the deployment of  

[7:8:66,587] Military Department personnel and equipment to support civil authority when called to state service by the Governor due to  

[7:9:66,597] domestic emergency or natural disaster and to provide the state, county, city, and other public agencies with the coordination  

[7:10:66,607] necessary to ensure a timely, organized response. The State Guard is a volunteer component of the Military Department whose  

[7:11:66,617] mission is to provide a trained, disciplined and ready force during training, preparation for mobilization, demobilization, and  

[7:12:66,627] provision of support to civil authorities during periods of state emergencies. The federal government provides virtually all  

[7:13:66,638] supplies, equipment, transportation, subsistence, and support services necessary for training, deployment, and commitment of  

[7:14:66,648] the National Guard. Training is conducted in accordance with the Department of the Army and Air Force Regulations and  

[7:15:66,658] Training Guidance.  

[8:0:66,676] 6912 - YOUTH AND COMMUNITY PROGRAMS  

[9:0:66,693] The Military Department manages and maintains partnerships with the following ten programs while serving more than 13,000  

[9:1:66,704] youth annually: California Cadet Corps, Oakland Military Institute, California Military Institute, Porterville Military Academy,  

[9:2:66,714] Grizzly Youth Academy, Sunburst Youth Academy, Discovery ChalleNGe Academy, California Jobs ChalleNGe, STARBASE  

[10:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] GENERAL GOVERNMENT 

[1:0:416,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — GG   93 

[2:0:196,32] 8940   Military Department - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] Academy Sacramento and STARBASE Academy Los Alamitos. The California Cadet Corps, founded in 1911 by the California  

[3:1:66,79] Legislature and then- Adjutant General BG Edwin Forbes, is a school-based, applied leadership program that is designed to  

[3:2:66,89] provide maximum growth and leadership opportunities for cadets from elementary through high school levels. It provides  

[3:3:66,99] leadership opportunities for cadets by allowing them to conduct training for junior cadets, perform as leaders in their cadet  

[3:4:66,109] military units, and by demonstrating proper behavior and citizenship at their schools and in their communities. As part of their  

[3:5:66,120] training, cadets provide assistance and support to the school and community. The Oakland Military Institute, California Military  

[3:6:66,130] Institute, and Porterville Military Academy develops leaders of character by providing rigorous programs to promote excellence  

[3:7:66,140] in academics, leadership, citizenship, athletics, and physical fitness/wellbeing. Additionally, the programs instill the 10 Cadet  

[3:8:66,150] Corps values of loyalty, education, ambition, duty, enthusiasm, respect, service, health, integrity, and personal courage. Using a  

[3:9:66,160] military framework, these programs graduate cadets who are capable of meeting the admissions requirements for college and  

[3:10:66,171] who are prepared for their roles as future leaders. The mission of the National Guard Youth ChalleNGe Program is to intervene  

[3:11:66,181] in and reclaim the lives of 16-18 year old high school dropouts. Graduates leave the program with the values, life skills,  

[3:12:66,191] education, and self-discipline necessary to succeed as productive citizens. California Jobs ChalleNGe Located at Los Alamitos,  

[3:13:66,201] CA is a continuation program to provide vocational skills to California ChalleNGe graduates. The overall mission provides post-

[3:14:66,211] secondary Career Technical Education (CTE) training to place graduates into viable jobs. The goal of the Department of  

[3:15:66,222] Defense STARBASE program is to motivate elementary school students, primarily 5th graders, to explore Science, Technology,  

[3:16:66,232] Engineering and Math (STEM) as they continue their education. The curriculum is designed to increase student involvement  

[3:17:66,242] and interest in STEM, enhance their understanding of the role that STEM literacy plays in their lives, strengthen potential for  

[3:18:66,252] future careers, and make the pursuit of STEM activities more attractive and accessible. While attending STARBASE students  

[3:19:66,262] interact with military personnel to explore careers and observe STEM applications in the "real world." These ten youth  

[3:20:66,273] programs are financed with federal, state and local funds. The California National Guard is involved in youth programs because  

[3:21:66,283] political and community leaders at the federal, state, and local levels recognize that the National Guard brings structure,  

[3:22:66,293] discipline, and effective leadership training methods to the educational setting.

[8:0:66,360] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[9:0:66,379] The Army National Guard statewide facilities footprint includes 87 active armories comprised of over 1.98 million square  

[9:1:66,389] footage. In addition, the Military Department possesses 4 aviation centers, 29 field maintenance shops, 2 combined support  

[9:2:66,399] maintenance shops, and 2 maneuver area training equipment sites. The total real property assets of the Military Department  

[9:3:66,409] encompass an area of 6 million square feet. The facilities are used to house and train the California National Guard and  

[9:4:66,420] provide emergency public safety support. The Military Department also operates three major training facilities comprised of a  

[9:5:66,430] total combined square footage of 3.8 million square feet. These facilities consist of troop lodging, administration, warehouse,  

[9:6:66,440] maintenance, firing ranges, and maneuver training areas. Additionally, the Military Department's footprint includes 43 active  

[9:7:66,450] buildings of 206,241 square feet for the Department’s Youth and Community Programs.

[11:0:199,522] 8955   Department of Veterans Affairs  

[12:0:66,543] The California Department of Veterans Affairs (CalVet) honors and serves California's veterans and their families by ensuring  

[12:1:66,553] our veterans have access to the benefits they have earned to enable them to meet their needs. CalVet promotes and delivers  

[12:2:66,563] innovative services through efficient, cost-effective programs and collaboration with key stakeholders and partners.  

[13:0:66,584] More specifically, CalVet:  

[13:1:65,601] • Communicates with California's veterans and their families at all pivotal moments throughout their post-military lives to  

[13:2:76,612] educate them and connect them to their federal, state, and local benefits.  

[13:3:65,628] • Provides California veterans and their families with direct representation in presenting their claims and appeals for benefits  

[13:4:76,638] before the US Department of Veterans Affairs and the federal Board of Veteran Appeals.  

[13:5:65,654] • Provides California veterans the opportunity to achieve the American dream of homeownership through direct low-cost loans  

[13:6:76,664] to acquire farms and homes.  

[14:0:65,681] • Provides the state's aged or disabled veterans with premier rehabilitative, residential, and medical care and services in a  

[14:1:76,691] home-like environment at the California Veterans Homes.  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:2:66,89] assistance to returning veterans and their families, whose lives were interrupted when they responded to our country's call to  

[3:3:66,99] military service.

[4:1:66,130] this need.   For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[7:0:66,208] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[8:0:65,227] • California Veteran Health Initiative—The Budget includes $50 million one-time General Fund, to be spent over three years,  

[8:1:76,237] for the California Veteran Health Initiative to focus on prevention, early intervention, and direct services to effectively combat  

[8:2:76,247] the risk factors associated with suicidal idealization, thereby reducing the number of veterans that die by suicide through a  

[8:3:76,258] mental health support network, an outreach and education campaign, and a veteran suicide surveillance and review  

[8:4:76,268] program.  

[9:0:65,284] • Veterans Home of California-Yountville Emergency Power Connections—The Budget includes $5.3 million one-time General  

[9:1:76,294] Fund to install transfer switches and upgrade panels in seven residential buildings at the Veterans Home of California-

[9:2:76,304] Yountville. Total project costs include design, construction, project management, and associated project fees.  

[10:0:65,321] • Information Technology Refresh and Security—The Budget includes $1.8 million ongoing General Fund for information  

[10:1:76,331] technology investments. Specifically, the Budget includes $1.4 million ongoing to refresh hardware on a five-year cycle and  

[10:2:76,341] $365,000 in 2022-23, and $354,000 ongoing, to implement new security solutions and mitigate security risks and incidents.  

[11:0:65,357] • Administrative Support—The Budget includes $1.5 million ongoing General Fund to provide additional administrative support  

[11:1:76,367] for legal affairs services, purchasing and contracting, graphic design, clinical oversight, and financial operations.  

[12:0:65,384] • Facility Planning and Construction Management Staffing—The Budget includes $515,000 ongoing General Fund to manage  

[12:1:76,394] increased maintenance workload for the five most recently operationalized Veterans Homes.  

[13:0:65,410] • Cemetery Operations—The Budget includes $492,000 General Fund in 2022-23 and $483,000 ongoing to provide additional  

[13:1:76,420] custodial, support, and security staffing for cemetery and burial operations.  

[14:0:65,436] • Healthcare Records and Health Informatics—The Budget includes $1.1 million General Fund in 2022-23 and $635,000  

[14:1:76,447] ongoing for the Electronic Healthcare Records project and Health Informatics support. Specifically, the Budget includes  

[14:2:76,457] $433,000 in 2022-23 to implement Phase 3 of the Electronic Healthcare Records project that will replace several disparate  

[14:3:76,467] legacy healthcare information technology programs, and $657,000 General Fund in 2022-23, and $635,000 ongoing, for the  

[14:4:76,477] Health Informatics and Applications and Development Services units to provide additional support for information technology  

[14:5:76,487] infrastructure, data analytics, and maintenance of various information technology environments.

[5:0:66,418] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,438] 6990 - FARM AND HOME LOANS TO VETERANS  

[6:0:66,458] CalVet’s Home Loan Program offers veterans, meeting specified requirements, loans to purchase, construct, or rehabilitate new  

[6:1:66,468] or existing single family dwellings, which include condominiums, units in shared equity cooperative housing developments,  

[6:2:66,478] mobile homes, and farms. CalVet also offers Home Improvement Loans to active CalVet customers or homeowners who own  

[6:3:66,489] their homes free of debt.  

[6:4:66,506] 6995 - VETERANS CLAIMS AND RIGHTS  

[7:0:66,527] The Veteran Services Division provides service and assistance to California's veterans, dependents, and survivors. Programs  

[7:1:66,537] administered consist of: Veterans Dependents Educational Assistance Program, County Veterans Service Office Program,  

[7:2:66,547] Veterans Outreach Program, California Transition Assistance Program, Veterans Housing and Homelessness Prevention  

[7:3:66,557] program, Medi-Cal Cost Avoidance Program, Veterans Claims and Appeals Representation, State Veteran Cemeteries,  

[7:4:66,568] California State Approving Agency for Veterans Education, Disabled Veteran Business Enterprise Program, Veterans Mental  

[7:5:66,578] Health Program including Veteran Justice Outreach and Incarcerated Veterans, Homeless Veterans Outreach Program,  

[7:6:66,588] Veterans Driver License Program, and the Veterans License Plate Program. California, with approximately 1.6 million veterans,  

[7:7:66,598] represents 8.4 percent of the nation's total veteran population.  

[7:8:66,616] 7000 - CARE OF SICK AND DISABLED VETERANS  

[8:0:66,634] CalVet operates eight licensed long-term care facilities which provide affordable, quality services to eligible veterans and  

[8:1:66,644] spouses. The Veterans Homes also provide therapeutic care and rehabilitative services to help   sustain activities of daily living  

[8:2:66,654] and independent function. The primary services provided by these Veterans Homes are:  

[8:3:65,672] • The Veterans Home of California, Barstow offers skilled nursing, intermediate, and domiciliary care.  

[8:4:65,688] • The Veterans Home of California, Chula Vista offers skilled nursing, assisted living, and domiciliary care.  

[8:5:65,704] • The Veterans Home of California, Fresno offers skilled nursing (including memory care) and assisted living care.  

[9:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:65,69] • The Veterans Home of California, Greater Los Angeles and Ventura County (GLAVC), includes three main campuses: West  

[3:1:76,79] Los Angeles (WLA), Lancaster, and Ventura. All three offer assisted living care. The WLA campus also offers skilled nursing  

[3:2:76,89] care (including memory care).  

[4:0:65,105] • The Veterans Home of California, Redding offers skilled nursing (including memory care) and assisted living care.  

[4:1:65,121] • The Veterans Home of California, Yountville is the largest veterans home in the United States. It offers all four levels of care:  

[4:2:76,132] skilled nursing (including memory care), intermediate, assisted living, and domiciliary care.  

[4:3:66,149] 7005 - VETERANS MEMORIALS  

[5:0:66,170] This program supports the Veterans Registry, which is part of the California Veterans Memorial. The provided contributions help  

[5:1:66,180] to defray the costs of data entry and system management for the Registry and the reasonable costs that are incurred by CalVet  

[5:2:66,190] for administering the fund.  

[5:3:66,208] 9900 - GENERAL ADMINISTRATION  

[6:0:66,226] Administration provides CalVet's full range of programs with administrative services support in the areas of legislation,  

[6:1:66,236] communications, legal, women veterans issues, minority veterans issues, information services, budgeting, accounting,  

[6:2:66,246] personnel, business services, program review and audits, corporate compliance, regulations, and emergency coordination.  

[6:3:66,256] These costs are distributed to the major programs.

[6:0:66,221] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[7:0:66,240] The Department of Veterans Affairs (CalVet) facilities include eight veteran’s homes, three state veterans’ cemeteries, and a  

[7:1:66,250] headquarters office building. The eight veteran’s homes are located in Yountville, Barstow, Chula Vista, West Los Angeles,  

[7:2:66,261] Lancaster, Ventura, Redding, and Fresno on 766 acres of land, with 2.4 million gross square feet of building space.  

[7:3:66,271] Additionally, CalVet is constructing a new 285,000 square foot Skilled Nursing Facility in Yountville, California and is expanding  

[7:4:66,281] columbarium capacity at the Northern California Veterans Cemetery in Igo, California. Veteran’s homes provide domiciliary  

[7:5:66,291] housing for the state's veterans and are also licensed to provide three levels of care, ranging from assisted living to skilled  

[7:6:66,301] nursing. CalVet also operates three cemeteries that are located in Igo, near Redding, Yountville, and Seaside and contain a  

[7:7:66,312] total of approximately 24,000 gravesites on 47 developed acres.

[11:0:259,482] 9100   Tax Relief  

[12:0:66,503] This budget provides funding for cities and counties to help defray the loss of revenue as a result of the Homeowners' Property  

[12:1:66,513] Tax Relief Program, a program that provides California homeowners assistance through a $7,000 exemption from property tax,  

[12:2:66,524] and the Open Space Subventions Program, a program that provides tax relief to individuals who agreed to hold their land as  

[12:3:66,534] open space under the Williamson Act of 1965.   This budget also receives loan repayments from people participating in the  

[12:4:66,544] Senior Citizens and Disabled Citizens Property Tax Postponement Program.

[7:1:66,228] Revenue and Taxation Code sections 421-430.5.  

[8:0:66,275] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:1:66,294] 7500 - HOMEOWNERS' PROPERTY TAX RELIEF  

[9:0:66,315] In order to reduce California taxpayers' property tax burden, the California Constitution exempts homeowners from paying  

[9:1:66,325] property taxes on the first $7,000 of the assessed value of their principal place of residence. The Constitution requires the state  

[9:2:66,335] to reimburse local governments for revenues lost due to the homeowners' exemption.   This Program also receives repayments  

[9:3:66,345] from persons participating in the Senior Citizens and Disabled Citizens Property Tax Postponement Program, whereby the  

[9:4:66,356] state makes property tax payments for qualified homeowners in exchange for the participants making repayment when they  

[9:5:66,366] relinquish ownership.  

[9:6:66,384] 7505 - SUBVENTIONS FOR OPEN SPACE  

[10:0:66,404] The Williamson Act permits cities and counties to enter into contracts with landowners to limit the use of land to agricultural,  

[10:1:66,414] scenic, and open space purposes. Article XIII, section 8, of the California Constitution permits land under such restrictions to be  

[10:2:66,424] assessed at other than market value based on its limited uses. The state provides reimbursements to cities and counties to  

[10:3:66,435] partially defray the loss of property tax revenues. The Department of Conservation administers this program.

[6:0:171,205] 9285   Trial Court Security - Court Construction  

[7:0:66,226] This budget establishes a process and mechanism for counties to request funding if court construction projects, occupied on or  

[7:1:66,236] after October 9, 2011, modify or create building features that increase overall trial court security costs. Trial Court Security was  

[7:2:66,247] realigned from the state to counties through 2011 Public Safety Realignment. Proposition 30, passed by the voters in 2012,  

[7:3:66,257] requires the state to provide annual funding for newly required activities that have an overall effect of increasing county costs in  

[7:4:66,267] this realigned program.

[10:0:171,488] 9620   Cash Management and Budgetary Loans  

[10:1:66,509] The Cash Management and Budgetary Loans budget tracks General Fund expenditures for interest costs related to cash flow  

[10:2:66,520] and budgetary borrowing. Internal and external cash flow borrowing can be used to overcome normal cash flow imbalances  

[10:3:66,530] during the fiscal year. Because receipts and disbursements occur unevenly throughout the fiscal year, the General Fund may  

[10:4:66,540] need to borrow even though its budget is balanced. Budgetary loans may be used to loan monies from various state funds to  

[10:5:66,550] the General Fund to help bridge a budget shortfall. These loans are temporary, and do not harm the operations of departmental  

[10:6:66,560] programs.

[10:0:66,362] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:0:66,382] 7720 - CASH MANAGEMENT  

[12:0:66,402] Borrowing From Other Funds or Other State/Local Entities  

[13:0:66,422] Chapter 312, Statutes of 1907, authorized the transfers to the General Fund from certain specified funds (often referred to as  

[13:1:66,433] "borrowable" funds) in the State Treasury System whenever the General Fund was exhausted. Those provisions, in slightly  

[13:2:66,443] modified form, are now contained in Sections 16310 and 16418 of the Government Code. This authorization enables the  

[13:3:66,453] General Fund to overcome normal cash flow imbalances throughout the fiscal year to a large extent. The use of these funds'  

[13:4:66,463] money for General Fund cash flow purposes is largely transparent to most of these funds. This mechanism does not interfere  

[13:5:66,473] with the day-to-day cash needs of the borrowable funds because of the implementation method. Generally, when a fund is  

[13:6:66,484] designated as borrowable for General Fund cash flow purposes, it merely increases the amount of cash the State Controller's  

[13:7:66,494] Office is authorized to borrow from the Pooled Money Investment Account (PMIA) when there is a General Fund cash  

[13:8:66,504] shortage. For most of these funds, actual cash is not transferred out of the funds into the General Fund. The entire cash  

[13:9:66,514] balances of these funds remain intact and completely available for the funds' purposes.  

[14:0:66,535] A State Agency Investment Fund (SAIF) Program was enacted pursuant to Chapter 142, Statutes of 2011 (Senate Bill 79,  

[14:1:66,545] Government Code section 16330), to increase internal borrowable resources through the receipt of deposits from a state entity  

[14:2:66,555] that is not required to deposit or invest funds in the PMIA. The minimum deposit or investment per participant is $500 million  

[14:3:66,565] and the maximum amount for the SAIF Program for all participants is $10 billion. There are currently no deposits in this  

[14:4:66,575] Program.  

[15:0:66,596] A Voluntary Investment Program (VIP) was enacted pursuant to Chapter 44, Statutes of 2012 (Senate Bill 1033, Government  

[15:1:66,606] Code section 16340), for the receipt of voluntary deposits from local entities for the purpose of providing additional liquidity for  

[15:2:66,616] the state's cash management. The minimum deposit from a local entity is $200 million and the maximum amount for the VIP  

[15:3:66,626] from all local entities combined is $10 billion. No deposits have been made into this Program.  

[16:0:66,657] Borrowing From Financial Markets  

[17:0:66,677] In addition, the courts have validated temporary external borrowing that meets the "appropriation doctrine." Under this doctrine,  

[17:1:66,688] an obligation is not considered a debt or liability within the State Constitutional limitation on indebtedness (Section 1 of Article  

[17:2:66,698] XVI) if an appropriation is made from existing funds or reasonably anticipated funds subject to appropriation. This doctrine was  

[17:3:66,708] invoked in 1933 and 1936 to uphold the use of registered warrants (IOUs) during the Great Depression and again in 1971 to  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.
