[2:0:136,32] 9620   Cash Management and Budgetary Loans - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] validate the State of California Notes provisions of Chapter 223, Statutes of 1971. The Notes provisions were invoked in  

[3:1:66,79] 1971-72 in lieu of the more costly registered warrant authority used during the Depression and, like registered warrants,  

[3:2:66,89] required the projected exhaustion of all internal sources of funds before being implemented. The Notes provisions were re-

[3:3:66,99] enacted by Chapter 10X, Statutes of 1983, and employed in 1982-83 and 1983-84.  

[4:0:66,120] In 1984-85, the state implemented a cash management program pursuant to Chapter 268, Statutes of 1984, which provided the  

[4:1:66,130] flexibility to borrow from external sources prior to exhausting internal sources. Under this program, the use of external funds  

[4:2:66,140] results in potential savings to the General Fund, as well as increased revenue to the various special funds from which the  

[4:3:66,150] General Fund would have borrowed from pursuant to Sections 16310 and 16418 of the Government Code.  

[5:0:66,171] External sources of borrowing available to the state include revenue anticipation notes (RANs), revenue anticipation warrants  

[5:1:66,181] (RAWs), and registered warrants (short-term IOUs). RANs and RAWs are short-term debt obligations issued in anticipation of  

[5:2:66,191] receiving revenues in the near future.  

[6:0:66,211] Additional costs to the cash management program include, but are not limited to, costs to issue and redeem IOUs, and any  

[6:1:66,222] other program measures necessary to effectively manage cash flow.  

[7:0:66,252] Overall Program  

[8:0:66,273] Pursuant to Government Code Sections 12020 and 12021, accounts payable and receivable and cash flow statements for the  

[8:1:66,283] past, current, and budget years are included as Schedule 5 in the Governor's Budget Summary. Neither cash receipts nor  

[8:2:66,293] disbursements match revenues and expenditures presented elsewhere in this Budget. To reflect cash flow, budget data is  

[8:3:66,303] converted to cash basis primarily by adjusting for cash collected by an agency but not yet recorded by the Controller and for  

[8:4:66,313] accruals. Adjustments are also made to reflect statutory changes, anticipated legislative actions, and administrative actions.  

[9:0:66,334] The estimated current and budget year cash flows incorporate the latest revenue and expenditure budget measures and  

[9:1:66,344] assumptions. During difficult cash periods, cash management measures such as intra-year payment deferrals may be used  

[9:2:66,354] from time-to-time to address low points during the fiscal year.  

[9:3:66,372] 7725 - BUDGETARY LOANS  

[10:0:66,390] The Budget Act authorizes budgetary loans from various funds and accounts to the General Fund. These loans are repaid over  

[10:1:66,400] multiple fiscal years. Unless otherwise specified in statute, the loans are repaid with interest calculated at the Pooled Money  

[10:2:66,410] Investment Account rate on the date of transfer.

[9:0:157,390] 9625   Interest Payments to the Federal Government  

[10:0:66,412] Pursuant to the Cash Management Improvement Act of 1990 (CMIA), the state will incur an interest liability to the federal  

[10:1:66,422] government. The purpose of CMIA is to ensure greater efficiency, effectiveness, and equity in the exchange of funds between  

[10:2:66,432] the federal government and the state. The major provisions of CMIA are: (a) federal agencies must make timely fund  

[10:3:66,442] disbursements and grant awards to the state; (b) the state must minimize the time between the deposit of federal funds in the  

[10:4:66,453] state account and the payout of the funds for program purposes; (c) the state is entitled to interest from the federal government  

[10:5:66,463] from the time state funds are paid out for program purposes until federal funds are deposited in the state account; and (d) the  

[10:6:66,473] federal government is entitled to interest from the state from the time federal funds are deposited in the state account until the  

[10:7:66,483] funds are paid out for program purposes.  

[11:0:66,504] The federal assistance programs impacted by the CMIA are those programs that have $285 million or more in federal fund  

[11:1:66,514] expenditures. For the majority of these programs, state departments request federal funds in advance of the warrant (i.e.,  

[11:2:66,524] check) issuance. State departments use this funding technique because the State Constitution requires that the funds be  

[11:3:66,534] deposited before the warrants are issued.  

[12:0:66,555] Interest payments to the federal government are due no later than March 31 each year. The payment will be for the interest  

[12:1:66,565] liability incurred during the state's prior fiscal year.

[1:0:168,69] 9650   Health and Dental Benefits for Annuitants  

[2:0:66,91] This program provides funding for health and dental benefit services for retired state employees and their dependents.  

[3:0:66,111] The program began on January 1, 1962, with an employer contribution of $5 per month toward the cost of a basic health plan.  

[3:1:66,121] Since then, major medical plans, Medicare, and plans supplementing Medicare have been developed. Dental care was added  

[3:2:66,131] in 1982.  

[4:0:66,152] The 2021-22 employer contribution for health premiums for employees hired by the state on and prior to December 31, 2016,  

[4:1:66,162] maintains the average 100/90 percent contribution formula established in Government Code Section 22871 for fully vested  

[4:2:66,172] members. Under this formula, the state averages the premiums of the four health benefit plans with the largest state enrollment  

[4:3:66,182] in order to calculate the maximum amount the state contributes towards retiree health benefits. The state also contributes 90  

[4:4:66,193] percent of this average towards the health benefit costs of each of the retiree's dependents. Vesting schedules and employer  

[4:5:66,203] contributions may vary by employee bargaining unit contract.  

[5:0:66,223] The 2021-22 employer contribution for health premiums for employees hired by the state on and after January 1, 2017,  

[5:1:66,233] maintains the average 80/80 percent contribution formula established in Government Code Section 22871.3 for fully vested  

[5:2:66,244] members. Under this formula, the state averages the premiums of the four health benefit plans with the largest state enrollment  

[5:3:66,254] in order to calculate the maximum amount the state contributes towards retiree health benefits. The state also contributes 80  

[5:4:66,264] percent of this average towards the health benefit costs of each of the retiree's dependents. Vesting schedules and employer  

[5:5:66,274] contributions may vary by employee bargaining unit contract.  

[6:0:66,295] The retiree is responsible for paying all health benefit plan costs that exceed the state contribution. Premiums are determined  

[6:1:66,305] on a calendar-year basis; therefore, the state contribution will vary during the fiscal year. The 2021 monthly contribution  

[6:2:66,315] maximums are $798 for a single enrollee, $1,519 for an enrollee and one dependent, and $1,937 for an enrollee and two or  

[6:3:66,325] more dependents. The 2022 monthly contribution maximums are $816 for a single enrollee, $1,548 for an enrollee and one  

[6:4:66,335] dependent, and $1,983 for an enrollee and two or more dependents. Dental care premiums vary by plan and number of  

[6:5:66,346] dependents.  

[7:0:66,366] Effective July 1, 2012, funding for health benefits for California State University annuitants is displayed in Organization Code  

[7:1:66,376] 6645 under Higher Education. This funding was previously budgeted within Organization Code 9650.

[6:0:204,216] 9658   Budget Stabilization Account  

[6:1:66,237] Proposition 2, approved by voters in November 2014, replaced the Proposition 58 (2004) version of the Budget Stabilization  

[6:2:66,247] Account (BSA) to build a stronger "rainy day" reserve while requiring accelerated debt pay down. Proposition 2 strengthened  

[6:3:66,258] the reserve by providing more stringent requirements to transfer funds back to the General Fund or suspend deposits to the  

[6:4:66,268] BSA, and increasing the reserve limit.  

[7:0:66,288] Proposition 2 requires a transfer from the General Fund to the BSA by October 1 of each year, as well as true up transfers  

[7:1:66,298] between the General Fund and the BSA for the two preceding fiscal years. In addition to the transfer to the BSA, a calculated  

[7:2:66,309] amount is appropriated for supplemental debt payments and other specified long-term liabilities.  

[8:0:66,336] LEGAL CITATIONS AND AUTHORITY  

[8:1:66,355] PROGRAM AUTHORITY  

[9:0:66,375] Section 20 and Section 22, Article XVI of the Constitution of the State of California.  

[9:1:66,385] Control Section 35.50, Budget Act.  

[9:2:66,403] Item 9658-011-0001, 2016 Budget Act and 2018 Budget Act.  

[10:0:66,440] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:0:66,460] 7760 - Proposition 2 requires the Legislature, through the 2029-30 fiscal year, to annually set aside in the BSA for a "rainy day"  

[11:1:66,470] 50 percent of the sum of 1.5 percent of estimated General Fund revenues and the amount of revenue from capital gains over 8  

[11:2:66,480] percent of General Fund tax proceeds that is not required to fund Proposition 98, and use the remaining 50 percent to pay  

[11:3:66,490] down debt. Beginning in 2030-31, 100 percent of the above sum would be deposited in the BSA for a "rainy day," reduced by  

[11:4:66,500] any amount up to 50 percent that the Legislature appropriates to pay down debt. The maximum balance in the BSA is limited to  

[11:5:66,511] 10 percent of General Fund proceeds of taxes for the fiscal year. Any amounts in excess of ten percent may only be used for  

[11:6:66,521] infrastructure needs.  

[12:0:66,541] Proposition 2 includes a true up mechanism for the two preceding years.  

[13:0:66,562] The Legislature may suspend or reduce the BSA transfer if the Governor declares a budget emergency. Withdrawal of funds  

[13:1:66,572] from the BSA is limited to half of the fund's balance unless funds in the BSA have been returned to the General Fund for  

[13:2:66,582] appropriation in the immediately preceding fiscal year.  

[14:0:66,602] Proposition 2 also establishes a Proposition 98 reserve whereby spikes in funding will be saved for future years. This will  

[14:1:66,613] smooth school spending and minimize future cuts. The Proposition 98 reserve does not change the Proposition 98 minimum  

[14:2:66,623] guarantee calculation.   See 9889 - Public School System Stabilization Account for more information on the Proposition 98  

[14:3:66,633] reserve.  

[15:0:66,673] FUND CONDITION STATEMENTS

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[6:0:66,227] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:1:66,246] 7765 - EQUITY CLAIMS  

[7:0:66,267] This program includes all claims approved by the three-member California Victim Compensation Board and the Government  

[7:1:66,277] Claims Program under the Department of General Services, and referred to the Legislature in the omnibus claims bills for  

[7:2:66,287] payment. The program provides an equitable procedure for the payment of the following categories of claims:  

[7:3:65,305] • Claims for which no appropriation has been made or for which no fund is available, but the settlement of which has been  

[7:4:76,315] provided for by statute or constitutional provision.  

[7:5:65,331] • Claims for which the appropriation made or fund designated is exhausted.  

[7:6:65,348] • Claims for which settlement is not otherwise provided for by statute or constitutional provision.  

[7:7:66,365] The Government Claims Program was transferred to the Department of General Services from the former California Victim  

[7:8:66,375] Compensation and Government Claims Board in July 2016. The California Victim Compensation and Government Claims  

[7:9:66,386] Board became the California Victim Compensation Board.  

[7:10:66,403] 7770 - SETTLEMENTS AND JUDGMENTS  

[8:0:66,424] This program includes claims paid through judgment and settlement bills sponsored by the Department of Justice each year.  

[8:1:66,434] The claims are either judgments against the state or settlements approved by the Department of Justice pursuant to the  

[8:2:66,444] authority provided in Government Code Section 948 et seq.

[8:0:164,402] 9800   Augmentation for Employee Compensation  

[9:0:66,423] This item reflects funding augmentations for state employee compensation. If the Legislature previously appropriated money,  

[9:1:66,433] through this budget item, to pay for the economic terms of employee compensation in previous fiscal years, the funding for  

[9:2:66,443] those economic terms is included in departments' budgets. When economic terms require funding not yet appropriated by the  

[9:3:66,454] Legislature, those funds are included in this budget item.  

[10:0:66,474] Additionally, this item sets aside funding for penalties the state may face under the Affordable Care Act (ACA). Beginning in  

[10:1:66,484] 2015, the state became subject to the Employer Shared Responsibility provision of the ACA. This provision requires large  

[10:2:66,494] employers to offer health benefits to full-time employees, defined as those who work on average 130 or more hours per month  

[10:3:66,505] (or 30 hours per week), and their dependent children. Under this provision, the state may be subject to penalties (Part B  

[10:4:66,515] Penalty), which may include part-time, temporary, and seasonal employees that may have been considered full-time for one or  

[10:5:66,525] more months, were not offered or did not qualify for health benefits through the state, purchased health coverage from the  

[10:6:66,535] Health Insurance Marketplace (known as Covered California), and received a premium tax credit or cost sharing reduction.  

[11:0:66,556] The ACA also contains reporting requirements, which require the state to track specified ACA-related employee information on  

[11:1:66,566] a monthly basis to identify the employees who meet health eligibility requirements, document whether benefits were offered,  

[11:2:66,576] and whether the employee accepted benefits. This data collection is used to report the state's level of ACA compliance to the  

[11:3:66,586] Internal Revenue Service on an annual basis. The state may be assessed penalties for inaccurate reporting (Internal Revenue  

[11:4:66,596] Code sections 6721 and 6722).

[7:1:66,275] Each year, the Budget Act includes appropriation items in business unit 9840 to be used to supplement department's  

[7:2:66,285] appropriations that are insufficient due to unanticipated expenditures or emergency situations. There are three separate  

[7:3:66,295] appropriations, one for each fund type - General, special, and non-governmental cost funds. These appropriations are allocated  

[7:4:66,305] to other departments by the Department of Finance based upon the determination of need.

[14:0:168,566] 9885   Reserve for Liquidation of Encumbrances  

[14:1:66,587] For individual department budgets, encumbrances (commitments for the procurement of goods or services which have not yet  

[14:2:66,597] been received by the state) are accrued as expenditures by departments at year-end and included in the expenditure totals.  

[14:3:66,608] This treatment is in accordance with the budgetary/legal basis of accounting and consistent with individual department budgets.  

[15:0:66,628] For the purpose of determining the overall General Fund balance, Government Code Section 13307 requires that  

[15:1:66,638] encumbrances should not be counted as a budgetary expenditure until the delivery of the goods and services. Furthermore,  

[15:2:66,648] Generally Accepted Accounting Principles (GAAP) require that encumbrances be reflected as a reserve against the General  

[15:3:66,659] Fund balance and not as an expenditure, as in budgetary/legal basis accounting. Government Code Section 13306 and state  

[15:4:66,669] policy require compliance with GAAP whenever it is in the best interest of the state. This budget reflects a statewide adjustment  

[15:5:66,679] to reduce expenditures on a budgetary/legal basis by the encumbrance amounts to comply with Government Code Section  

[15:6:66,689] 13307 and GAAP. A reserve of fund balance for encumbrances for this same amount is shown in Summary Schedule 1 for the  

[15:7:66,699] General Fund.  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] COMPUTATION OF ENCUMBRANCE ADJUSTMENT  

[4:0:66,89] The State Controller's Office accumulated a General Fund encumbrance total of $4,276,406,000 from 2019-20 year-end  

[4:1:66,99] financial statements submitted by state departments. For budgeting purposes, encumbrances are estimated to be at the same  

[4:2:66,109] level for the following fiscal years and are assumed to be liquidated (paid) within the next fiscal year.   Upon completion of the  

[4:3:66,120] year-end financial reports by the State Controller's Office, the budget information will be updated in the spring.   The  

[4:4:66,130] Encumbrance Adjustment Table summarizes the methodology and the calculation for the encumbrance adjustment.

[10:0:162,354] 9889   Public School System Stabilization Account  

[10:1:66,375] Proposition 2, approved by voters in November 2014, established the Public School System Stabilization Account (PSSSA) as  

[10:2:66,386] a ”rainy day“ reserve for K-14 schools.  

[11:0:66,403] Proposition 2 requires Proposition 98 General Fund deposits into or withdrawals from the fund each year, as well as two  

[11:1:66,413] preceding fiscal years, when specific criteria are met.   Calculation of a deposit or withdrawal from the   PSSSA does not change  

[11:2:66,424] the Proposition 98 minimum guarantee calculation.

[10:0:122,404] 9900   Statewide General Administrative Expenditures (Pro Rata)  

[11:0:66,425] Central service agencies, such as Department of Finance, the State Treasurer, the State Controller, and Legislature, provide  

[11:1:66,435] budgeting, banking, accounting, auditing, payroll, and other services to all state departments. The Pro Rata process apportions  

[11:2:66,446] the costs of providing central administrative services to all state departments and funding sources that benefit from the  

[11:3:66,456] services. Amounts apportioned to special funds for their fair share of central administrative services costs are transferred from  

[11:4:66,466] the special funds to the General Fund and the Central Service Cost Recovery Fund.  

[12:0:66,486] Prior to 2016-17, Pro Rata assessments to special funds were normally included in the appropriate departmental budgets. This  

[12:1:66,497] budget item included Pro Rata transfers directly assessed to special funds for those cases where appropriate funding was not  

[12:2:66,507] provided for in departmental budgets. Effective 2016-17, this budget item includes Pro Rata transfers assessed directly at the  

[12:3:66,517] fund level, rather than each individual appropriation, pursuant to Government Code section 11274.
